</div>
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Carbon capture and storage</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">  (Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide_recycling&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon dioxide recycling">Carbon dioxide recycling</a>)</span></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_utilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon capture and utilization">Carbon capture and utilization</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg/350px-Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg.png" width="350" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg/525px-Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg/700px-Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="530" data-file-height="420" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Schematic showing both terrestrial and geological sequestration of carbon dioxide emissions from a coal-fired plant</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Carbon capture and storage</b> (<b>CCS</b>) (or <b>carbon capture and sequestration</b>) is the process of capturing waste <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>) from large <a href="/wiki/Point_source_pollution" title="Point source pollution">point sources</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_power_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuel power plant">fossil fuel power plants</a>, transporting it to a storage site, and depositing it where it will not enter the atmosphere, normally an underground <a href="/wiki/Formation_(stratigraphy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Formation (stratigraphy)">geological formation</a>. The aim is to prevent the release of large quantities of CO<sub>2</sub> into the atmosphere (from fossil fuel use in power generation and other industries). It is a potential means of <a href="/wiki/Mitigation_of_global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Mitigation of global warming">mitigating</a> the contribution of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_emissions" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuel emissions">fossil fuel emissions</a> to <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a><sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Although CO<sub>2</sub> has been injected into geological formations for several decades for various purposes, including <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_oil_recovery" title="Enhanced oil recovery">enhanced oil recovery</a>, the long term storage of CO<sub>2</sub> is a relatively new concept. The first commercial example was the <a href="/wiki/Weyburn-Midale_Carbon_Dioxide_Project" title="Weyburn-Midale Carbon Dioxide Project">Weyburn-Midale Carbon Dioxide Project</a> in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Other examples include <a href="/wiki/SaskPower" title="SaskPower">SaskPower's</a> <a href="/wiki/Boundary_Dam_Power_Station" title="Boundary Dam Power Station">Boundary Dam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Power" title="Mississippi Power">Mississippi Power's</a> <a href="/wiki/Kemper_Project" title="Kemper Project">Kemper Project</a>. 'CCS' can also be used to describe the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_scrubber" title="Carbon dioxide scrubber">scrubbing</a> of CO<sub>2</sub> from ambient air as a <a href="/wiki/Climate_engineering" title="Climate engineering">climate engineering</a> technique.</p>
<p>An integrated pilot-scale CCS power plant was to begin operating in September 2008 in the eastern German power plant <a href="/wiki/Schwarze_Pumpe_power_station" title="Schwarze Pumpe power station">Schwarze Pumpe</a> run by utility <a href="/wiki/Vattenfall" title="Vattenfall">Vattenfall</a>, to test the technological feasibility and economic efficiency. CCS applied to a modern conventional power plant could reduce CO<sub>2</sub> emissions to the atmosphere by approximately 80–90% compared to a plant without CCS.<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_CC_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_CC-4">[4]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">IPCC</a> estimates that the economic potential of CCS could be between 10% and 55% of the total carbon mitigation effort until year 2100.<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_CC_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_CC-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carbon dioxide can be captured out of air or fossil fuel power plant <a href="/wiki/Flue_gas" title="Flue gas">flue gas</a> using <a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">adsorption</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_scrubber" title="Carbon dioxide scrubber">carbon scrubbing</a>), membrane gas separation, or adsorption technologies. Amines are the leading carbon scrubbing technology. Capturing and compressing CO<sub>2</sub> may increase the energy needs of a coal-fired CCS plant by 25–40%.<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_CC_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_CC-4">[4]</a></sup> These and other system costs are estimated to increase the cost per watt energy produced by 21–91% for fossil fuel power plants.<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_CC_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_CC-4">[4]</a></sup> Applying the technology to existing plants would be more expensive, especially if they are far from a sequestration site. A 2005 industry report suggests that with successful research, development and deployment (RD&amp;D), sequestered coal-based electricity generation in 2025 may cost less than unsequestered coal-based electricity generation today.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Storage of the CO<sub>2</sub> is envisaged either in deep geological formations, or in the form of <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">mineral</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonates</a>. Deep ocean storage is not currently considered feasible due to the associated effect of <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-greenfacts.org_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenfacts.org-6">[6]</a></sup> Geological formations are currently considered the most promising sequestration sites. The <a href="/wiki/National_Energy_Technology_Laboratory" title="National Energy Technology Laboratory">National Energy Technology Laboratory</a> (NETL) reported that North America has enough storage capacity for more than 900 years worth of carbon dioxide at current production rates.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> A general problem is that long term predictions about submarine or underground storage security are very difficult and uncertain, and there is still the risk that CO<sub>2</sub> might leak into the atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-51"><a href="#Environmental_effects"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Environmental effects</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-52"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-53"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-54"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-55"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Capture">Capture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Capture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_scrubber" title="Carbon dioxide scrubber">Carbon dioxide scrubber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_air_capture" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon dioxide air capture">carbon dioxide air capture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clean_coal" class="mw-redirect" title="Clean coal">Clean coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amine_gas_treating" title="Amine gas treating">Amine gas treating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Membrane_gas_separation" title="Membrane gas separation">Membrane gas separation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Metal-organic_framework" title="Metal-organic framework">Metal-organic framework</a></div>
<p>Capturing CO<sub>2</sub> is most effective at point sources, such as large fossil fuel or biomass energy facilities, industries with major CO<sub>2</sub> emissions, <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_processing" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural gas processing">natural gas processing</a>, synthetic fuel plants and fossil fuel-based <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_production" title="Hydrogen production">hydrogen production</a> plants. Extracting CO<sub>2</sub> from air is also possible, but not very practical because the CO<sub>2</sub> is not concentrated.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Organisms that produce <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> by <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">fermentation</a> generate cool, essentially pure CO<sub>2</sub> that can be pumped underground.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Fermentation produces slightly less CO<sub>2</sub> than ethanol by weight.</p>
<p>Flue gas from the combustion of coal in oxygen has a large concentration of CO2, about 10-15% CO<sub>2</sub> whereas natural gas power plant flue gas is about 5–10% CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-11">[11]</a></sup> Therefore, it is more energy and cost efficient to capture CO2 from coal-fired power plants. Impurities in CO<sub>2</sub> streams, like sulfurs and water, could have a significant effect on their phase behaviour and could pose a significant threat of increased corrosion of pipeline and well materials.[10] In instances where CO<sub>2</sub> impurities exist, especially with air capture, a scrubbing separation process would be needed to initially clean the flue gas.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> According to the Wallula Energy Resource Center in Washington state, by gasifying coal, it is possible to capture approximately 65% of carbon dioxide embedded in it and sequester it in a solid form.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Broadly, three different configurations of technologies for capture exist: post-combustion, pre-combustion, and oxyfuel combustion:</p>
<ul>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Post_combustion_capture" class="mw-redirect" title="Post combustion capture">post combustion capture</a></i>, the CO<sub>2</sub> is removed after combustion of the fossil fuel&#160;— this is the scheme that would be applied to fossil-fuel burning power plants. Here, carbon dioxide is captured from <a href="/wiki/Flue_gas" title="Flue gas">flue gases</a> at <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_power_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuel power plant">power stations</a> or other large point sources. The technology is well understood and is currently used in other industrial applications, although not at the same scale as might be required in a commercial scale power station. Post combustion capture is most popular in research because existing fossil fuel power plants can be retrofitted to include CCS technology in this configuration.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></li>
<li>The technology for <i>pre-combustion</i> is widely applied in fertilizer, chemical, gaseous fuel (H<sub>2</sub>, CH<sub>4</sub>), and power production.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> In these cases, the fossil fuel is partially oxidized, for instance in a <a href="/wiki/Gasification" title="Gasification">gasifier</a>. The resulting <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a> (CO and H<sub>2</sub>) is <a href="/wiki/Water_gas_shift_reaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Water gas shift reaction">shifted</a> into CO<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>. The resulting CO<sub>2</sub> can be captured from a relatively pure exhaust stream. The H<sub>2</sub> can now be used as fuel; the carbon dioxide is removed before combustion takes place. There are several advantages and disadvantages when compared to conventional post combustion carbon dioxide capture.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The CO<sub>2</sub> is removed after combustion of fossil fuels, but before the flue gas is expanded to atmospheric pressure. This scheme is applied to new fossil fuel burning power plants, or to existing plants where re-powering is an option. The capture before expansion, i.e. from pressurized gas, is standard in almost all industrial CO<sub>2</sub> capture processes, at the same scale as will be required for utility power plants.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Oxy-fuel_combustion" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxy-fuel combustion">oxy-fuel combustion</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> the fuel is burned in oxygen instead of air. To limit the resulting flame temperatures to levels common during conventional combustion, cooled flue gas is recirculated and injected into the combustion chamber. The flue gas consists of mainly carbon dioxide and water vapour, the latter of which is condensed through cooling. The result is an almost pure carbon dioxide stream that can be transported to the sequestration site and stored. Power plant processes based on oxyfuel combustion are sometimes referred to as "zero emission" cycles, because the CO<sub>2</sub> stored is not a fraction removed from the flue gas stream (as in the cases of pre- and post-combustion capture) but the flue gas stream itself. A certain fraction of the CO<sub>2</sub> generated during combustion will inevitably end up in the condensed water. To warrant the label "zero emission" the water would thus have to be treated or disposed of appropriately. The technique is promising, but the initial air separation step demands a lot of energy.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="CO2_separation_technologies">CO<sub>2</sub> separation technologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: CO2 separation technologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carbon dioxide can be separated out of air or flue gas with <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(chemistry)" title="Absorption (chemistry)">absorption</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">adsorption</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Membrane_gas_separation" title="Membrane gas separation">membrane gas separation</a> technologies. Absorption, or <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_scrubber" title="Carbon dioxide scrubber">carbon scrubbing</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Amines" class="mw-redirect" title="Amines">amines</a> is currently the dominant capture technology. Membrane and adsorption technologies are still in the developmental research stages, initiating primary pilot plants in the near future. <a href="/wiki/Metal-Organic_Framework" class="mw-redirect" title="Metal-Organic Framework">Metal-Organic Frameworks</a> (MOFs) are a novel class of materials that offer promise for carbon capture using adsorption technologies.</p>
<p>Carbon dioxide adsorbs to a MOF through <a href="/wiki/Physisorption" title="Physisorption">physisorption</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chemisorption" title="Chemisorption">chemisorption</a> based on the porosity and <a href="/wiki/Sensitivity_and_specificity" title="Sensitivity and specificity">selectivity</a> of the MOF leaving behind a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">Greenhouse gas</a> poor gas stream that is more environmentally friendly. The carbon dioxide is then stripped off the MOF using temperature swing adsorption (TSA) or pressure swing adsorption (PSA) so the MOF can be reused. Adsorbents and absorbents require regeneration steps where the CO<sub>2</sub> is removed from the sorbent or solution that collected it out of the flue gas in order for the sorbent or solution to be reused. Monoethanolamine (MEA) solutions, the leading amine for capturing CO<sub>2</sub>, have a heat capacity between 3-4 J/g K since they are mostly water.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Higher heat capacities add to the energy penalty in the solvent regeneration step. Thus, to optimize a MOF for carbon capture, low heat capacities and heats of adsorption are desired. Additionally, high working capacity and high selectivity are desirable in order to capture as much CO<sub>2</sub> as possible from the flue gas. However, there is an energy trade off with selectivity and energy expenditure.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> As the amount of CO<sub>2</sub> captured increases, the energy, and therefore cost, required to regenerate increases. A large drawback of using MOFs for CCS is the limitations imposed by their chemical and thermal stability.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Current research is looking to optimize MOF properties for CCS, but it has proven difficult to find these optimizations that also result in a stable MOF. Metal reservoirs are also a limiting factor to the potential success of MOFs.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Capture is attributed to about two thirds of the total cost of CCS, making it limit the wide-scale deployment of CCS technologies. To optimize a CO<sub>2</sub> capture process would significantly increase the feasibility of CCS since the transport and storage steps of CCS are rather mature technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>An alternate method under development is <a href="/wiki/Chemical_looping_combustion" title="Chemical looping combustion">chemical looping combustion</a> (CLC). Chemical looping uses a metal oxide as a solid oxygen carrier. Metal oxide particles react with a solid, liquid or gaseous fuel in a <a href="/wiki/Fluidization" title="Fluidization">fluidized bed</a> combustor, producing solid metal particles and a mixture of carbon dioxide and water vapor. The water vapor is condensed, leaving pure carbon dioxide, which can then be sequestered. The solid metal particles are circulated to another fluidized bed where they react with air, producing heat and regenerating metal oxide particles that are recirculated to the fluidized bed combustor. A variant of chemical looping is <a href="/wiki/Calcium_looping" title="Calcium looping">calcium looping</a>, which uses the alternating carbonation and then calcination of a <a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxide" title="Calcium oxide">calcium oxide</a> based carrier as a means of capturing CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>A few engineering proposals have been made for the more difficult task of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_removal" title="Carbon dioxide removal">removing CO<sub>2</sub> from the atmosphere</a> – a form of <a href="/wiki/Climate_engineering" title="Climate engineering">climate engineering</a> – but work in this area is still in its infancy. Capture costs are estimated to be higher than from point sources, but may be feasible for dealing with emissions from diffuse sources such as automobiles and aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The theoretically required energy for air capture is only slightly more than for capture from point sources. The additional costs come from the devices that use the natural air flow. Global Research Technologies demonstrated a pre-prototype of air capture technology in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>After capture, the CO<sub>2</sub> would have to be transported to suitable storage sites. This would most likely be done by pipeline, which is generally the cheapest form of transport. In 2008, there were approximately 5,800&#160;km of CO<sub>2</sub> pipelines in the United States, used to transport CO<sub>2</sub> to oil production fields where it is then injected into older fields to extract oil. The injection of CO<sub>2</sub> to produce oil is generally called <i><a href="/wiki/Enhanced_Oil_Recovery" class="mw-redirect" title="Enhanced Oil Recovery">Enhanced Oil Recovery</a></i> or EOR.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In addition, there are several pilot programs in various stages to test the long-term storage of CO<sub>2</sub> in non-oil producing geologic formations.</p>
<p>According to the Congressional Research Service, "There are important unanswered questions about pipeline network requirements, economic regulation, utility cost recovery, regulatory classification of CO<sub>2</sub> itself, and pipeline safety. Furthermore, because CO<sub>2</sub> pipelines for <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_oil_recovery" title="Enhanced oil recovery">enhanced oil recovery</a> are already in use today, policy decisions affecting CO<sub>2</sub> pipelines take on an urgency that is unrecognized by many. Federal classification of CO<sub>2</sub> as both a commodity (by the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Land_Management" title="Bureau of Land Management">Bureau of Land Management</a>) and as a pollutant (by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">Environmental Protection Agency</a>) could potentially create an immediate conflict which may need to be addressed not only for the sake of future CCS implementation, but also to ensure consistency of future CCS with CO<sub>2</sub> pipeline operations today."<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ships could also be utilized for transport where pipelines are not feasible. These methods are currently used for transporting CO<sub>2</sub> for other applications.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sequestration">Sequestration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Sequestration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sequestration" title="Carbon sequestration">Carbon sequestration</a></div>
<p>Various forms have been conceived for permanent storage of CO<sub>2</sub>. These forms include gaseous storage in various deep geological formations (including saline formations and exhausted gas fields), and solid storage by reaction of CO<sub>2</sub> with metal <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxides</a> to produce stable <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonates</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geological_storage">Geological storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Geological storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Also known as <i>geo-sequestration</i>, this method involves injecting carbon dioxide, generally in <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_fluid" title="Supercritical fluid">supercritical</a> form, directly into underground geological formations. <a href="/wiki/Oil_field" title="Oil field">Oil fields</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gas_field" class="mw-redirect" title="Gas field">gas fields</a>, saline formations, unmineable <a href="/wiki/Coal_seam" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal seam">coal seams</a>, and saline-filled basalt formations have been suggested as storage sites. Various physical (e.g., highly impermeable <a href="/wiki/Caprock" title="Caprock">caprock</a>) and geochemical trapping mechanisms would prevent the CO<sub>2</sub> from escaping to the surface.<sup id="cite_ref-Energy_Institute_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Energy_Institute-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>CO<sub>2</sub> is sometimes injected into declining oil fields to <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_oil_recovery" title="Enhanced oil recovery">increase oil recovery</a>. Approximately 30 to 50 million metric tonnes of CO<sub>2</sub> are injected annually in the United States into declining oil fields.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> This option is attractive because the geology of hydrocarbon reservoirs is generally well understood and storage costs may be partly offset by the sale of additional oil that is recovered.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Disadvantages of old oil fields are their geographic distribution and their limited capacity, as well as the fact that subsequent burning of the additional oil recovered will offset much or all of the reduction in CO<sub>2</sub> emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unmineable coal seams can be used to store CO<sub>2</sub> because the CO<sub>2</sub> molecules attach to the surface of coal. The technical feasibility, however, depends on the permeability of the coal bed. In the process of absorption the coal releases previously absorbed <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, and the methane can be recovered (<a href="/wiki/Enhanced_coal_bed_methane_recovery" title="Enhanced coal bed methane recovery">enhanced coal bed methane recovery</a>). The sale of the methane can be used to offset a portion of the cost of the CO<sub>2</sub> storage. Burning the resultant methane, however, would negate some of the benefit of sequestering the original CO<sub>2</sub>.</p>
<p>Saline formations contain highly mineralized brines, and have so far been considered of no benefit to humans. Saline aquifers have been used for storage of chemical waste in a few cases. The main advantage of saline aquifers is their large potential storage volume and their common occurrence. The major disadvantage of saline aquifers is that relatively little is known about them, especially compared to oil fields. To keep the cost of storage acceptable, the geophysical exploration may be limited, resulting in larger uncertainty about the aquifer structure. Unlike storage in oil fields or coal beds, no side product will offset the storage cost. Leakage of CO<sub>2</sub> back into the atmosphere may be a problem in saline aquifer storage. Current research shows, however, that <i>trapping mechanisms</i> such as structural trapping, residual trapping, solubility trapping and mineral trapping could immobilize the CO<sub>2</sub> underground and reduce the risk of leakage.<sup id="cite_ref-Energy_Institute_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Energy_Institute-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Enhanced_oil_recovery">Enhanced oil recovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Enhanced oil recovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Enhanced_oil_recovery" title="Enhanced oil recovery">Enhanced oil recovery</a> (EOR) is a generic term for techniques used to increase the amount of <a href="/wiki/Crude_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a> that can be extracted from an <a href="/wiki/Oil_field" title="Oil field">oil field</a>. In Carbon Capture &amp; Sequestration Enhanced Oil Recovery (CCS EOR), carbon dioxide is injected into an oil field to recover oil that is often never recovered using more traditional methods.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Crude_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Crude oil">Crude oil</a> development and production in U.S. oil reservoirs can include up to three distinct phases: primary, secondary, and tertiary (or enhanced) recovery.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> During primary recovery only about 10 percent of a <a href="/wiki/Oil_reservoir" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil reservoir">reservoir's</a> original oil in place is typically produced. Secondary recovery techniques extend a field's productive life generally by injecting water or gas to displace oil and drive it to a production <a href="/wiki/Wellbore" class="mw-redirect" title="Wellbore">wellbore</a>, resulting in the recovery of 20 to 40 percent of the original oil in place. However, with much of the easy-to-produce oil already recovered from U.S. oil fields, producers have attempted several tertiary, or <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_oil_recovery" title="Enhanced oil recovery">enhanced oil recovery</a> (EOR), techniques that offer prospects for ultimately producing 30 to 60 percent, or more, of the reservoir's original oil in place.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>An example of a project that will use CCS EOR is the <a href="/wiki/Kemper_Project" title="Kemper Project">Kemper Project</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>. Due to the Kemper Project's close proximity to oil fields, the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> <a href="/wiki/Byproduct" class="mw-redirect" title="Byproduct">byproduct</a> from producing <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> will be transported to the neighboring <a href="/wiki/Oil_fields" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil fields">oil fields</a> for <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_oil_recovery" title="Enhanced oil recovery">EOR</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ocean_storage">Ocean storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ocean storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the past, it was suggested that CO<sub>2</sub> could be stored in the oceans, but this would only exacerbate <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a> and has been made illegal under specific regulations. Ocean storage is no longer considered feasible.<sup id="cite_ref-greenfacts.org_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenfacts.org-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mineral_storage">Mineral storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Mineral storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In this process, CO<sub>2</sub> is <a href="/wiki/Exothermic" class="mw-redirect" title="Exothermic">exothermically</a> reacted with available metal oxides, which in turn produces stable carbonates (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Calcite" title="Calcite">calcite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesite" title="Magnesite">magnesite</a>). This process occurs naturally over many years and is responsible for a great amount of surface <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>. The idea of using <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a> has been promoted by the geochemist Prof. Schuiling.<sup id="cite_ref-Olivine_weathering_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olivine_weathering-38">[38]</a></sup> The reaction rate can be made faster, for example, with a <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> or by reacting at higher temperatures and/or pressures, or by pre-treatment of the minerals, although this method can require additional energy. The <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">IPCC</a> estimates that a power plant equipped with CCS using mineral storage will need 60–180% more energy than a power plant without CCS.<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_CC_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_CC-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>The economics of mineral carbonation at scale are now being tested in a world-first pilot plant project based in Newcastle, Australia. New techniques for mineral activation and reaction have been developed the GreenMag Group and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Newcastle_(Australia)" title="University of Newcastle (Australia)">University of Newcastle</a> and funded by the <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a> and Australian Governments to be operational by 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-www.dpi.nsw.gov.au_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-www.dpi.nsw.gov.au-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009 it was reported that scientists had mapped 6,000 square miles (16,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) of rock formations in the U.S. that could be used to store 500 years' worth of U.S. carbon dioxide emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> A study on mineral sequestration in the US states:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Carbon sequestration by reacting naturally occurring Mg and Ca containing minerals with CO<sub>2</sub> to form carbonates has many unique advantages. Most notabl[e] is the fact that carbonates have a lower energy state than CO<sub>2</sub>, which is why mineral carbonation is thermodynamically favorable and occurs naturally (e.g., the weathering of rock over geologic time periods). Secondly, the raw materials such as magnesium based minerals are abundant. Finally, the produced carbonates are unarguably stable and thus re-release of CO<sub>2</sub> into the atmosphere is not an issue. However, conventional carbonation pathways are slow under ambient temperatures and pressures. The significant challenge being addressed by this effort is to identify an industrially and environmentally viable carbonation route that will allow mineral sequestration to be implemented with acceptable economics.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The following table lists principal metal oxides of <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">Earth's Crust</a>. Theoretically, up to 22% of this mineral mass is able to form <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonates</a>.</p>

<p>Ultramafic mine tailings are a readily available source of fine-grained metal oxides that can act as artificial carbon sinks to reduce net greenhouse gas emissions in the mining industry.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Accelerating passive CO<sub>2</sub> sequestration via mineral carbonation may be achieved through microbial processes that enhance mineral dissolution and carbonate precipitation.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_requirements">Energy requirements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Energy requirements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The energy requirements of sequestration processes may be significant. In one paper, sequestration consumed 25 percent of the plant's rated 600-megawatt output capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><i>After adding CO<sub>2</sub> capture and compression, the capacity of the coal-fired power plant is reduced to 457 MW.</i></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Leakage">Leakage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Leakage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tml15-16_Nc.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Tml15-16_Nc.jpg/220px-Tml15-16_Nc.jpg" width="220" height="79" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Tml15-16_Nc.jpg/330px-Tml15-16_Nc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Tml15-16_Nc.jpg/440px-Tml15-16_Nc.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1277" data-file-height="458" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tml15-16_Nc.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Lake_Nyos" title="Lake Nyos">Lake Nyos</a> as it appeared fewer than two weeks after the eruption; August 29, 1986.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A major concern with CCS is whether leakage of stored CO<sub>2</sub> will compromise CCS as a climate change mitigation option. For well-selected, designed and managed geological storage sites, IPCC estimates that risks are comparable to those associated with current hydrocarbon activity.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> However, this finding is contested due to a lack of experience in such long term storage.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> CO<sub>2</sub> could be trapped for millions of years, and although some leakage occurs upwards through the soil, well selected storage sites are likely to retain over 99% of the injected CO<sub>2</sub> over 1000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Leakage through the injection pipe is a greater risk.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the injection pipe is usually protected with <a href="/wiki/Non-return_valve" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-return valve">non-return valves</a> to prevent release on a power outage, there is still a risk that the pipe itself could tear and leak due to the pressure. The <a href="/wiki/Berkel_en_Rodenrijs" title="Berkel en Rodenrijs">Berkel en Rodenrijs</a> incident in December 2008 was an example, where a modest release of CO<sub>2</sub> from a pipeline under a bridge resulted in the deaths of some ducks sheltering there.<sup id="cite_ref-eenden_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eenden-53">[53]</a></sup> In order to measure accidental carbon releases more accurately and decrease the risk of fatalities through this type of leakage, the implementation of CO<sub>2</sub> alert meters around the project perimeter has been proposed. Malfunction of a carbon dioxide industrial fire suppression system in a large warehouse released CO<sub>2</sub> and 14 citizens collapsed on the nearby public road. A release of CO<sub>2</sub> from a salt mine killed a person at distance of 300 meters.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1986 a large leakage of naturally sequestered CO<sub>2</sub> rose from <a href="/wiki/Lake_Nyos" title="Lake Nyos">Lake Nyos</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> and asphyxiated 1,700 people. While the carbon had been sequestered naturally, some point to the event as evidence for the potentially catastrophic effects of sequestering carbon artificially.<sup id="cite_ref-forbes.com_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forbes.com-55">[55]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Lake_Nyos" title="Lake Nyos">Lake Nyos</a> disaster resulted from a volcanic event, which very suddenly released as much as a cubic kilometre of CO<sub>2</sub> gas from a pool of naturally occurring CO<sub>2</sub> under the lake in a deep narrow valley. The location of this pool of CO<sub>2</sub> is not a place where man can inject or store CO<sub>2</sub>, and this pool was not known about nor monitored until after the occurrence of the natural disaster.</p>
<p>For ocean storage, the retention of CO<sub>2</sub> would depend on the depth. The IPCC estimates 30–85% of the sequestered carbon dioxide would be retained after 500 years for depths 1000–3000 m. Mineral storage is not regarded as having any risks of leakage. The IPCC recommends that limits be set to the amount of leakage that can take place. This might rule out deep ocean storage as an option.</p>
<p>At the conditions of the deeper oceans, (about 400 bar or 40 MPa, 280 K) water–CO<sub>2</sub>(l) mixing is <i>very</i> low (where carbonate formation/acidification is the rate limiting step), but the formation of water-CO<sub>2</sub> hydrates, a kind of solid water cage that surrounds the CO<sub>2</sub>, is favorable.</p>
<p>To further investigate the safety of CO<sub>2</sub> sequestration, Norway's <a href="/wiki/Sleipner_gas_field" title="Sleipner gas field">Sleipner gas field</a> can be studied, as it is the oldest plant that stores CO<sub>2</sub> on an industrial scale. According to an environmental assessment of the gas field which was conducted after ten years of operation, the author affirmed that geosequestration of CO<sub>2</sub> was the most definite form of permanent <i>geological</i> storage of CO<sub>2</sub>:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Available geological information shows absence of major tectonic events after the deposition of the Utsira formation [saline reservoir]. This implies that the geological environment is tectonically stable and a site suitable for carbon dioxide storage. The solubility trapping [is] the most permanent and secure form of geological storage.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In March 2009 StatoilHydro issued a study showing the slow spread of CO<sub>2</sub> in the formation after more than 10 years operation.<sup id="cite_ref-Energy-pedia_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Energy-pedia-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Phase I of the <a href="/wiki/Weyburn-Midale_Carbon_Dioxide_Project" title="Weyburn-Midale Carbon Dioxide Project">Weyburn-Midale Carbon Dioxide Project</a> in <a href="/wiki/Weyburn,_Saskatchewan" class="mw-redirect" title="Weyburn, Saskatchewan">Weyburn, Saskatchewan</a>, Canada has determined that the likelihood of stored CO<sub>2</sub> release is less than one percent in 5,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> A January 2011 report, however, claimed <a href="/wiki/Weyburn-Midale_Carbon_Dioxide_Project#Claims_of_leaking" title="Weyburn-Midale Carbon Dioxide Project">evidence of leakage</a> in land above that project.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> This report was strongly refuted by the IEAGHG Weyburn-Midale CO<sub>2</sub> Monitoring and Storage Project, which issued an eight-page analysis of the study, claiming that it showed no evidence of leakage from the reservoir.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>The liability of potential leak(s) is one of the largest barriers to large-scale CCS. To assess and reduce such liability, the leakage of stored gasses, particularly <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, into the atmosphere may be detected via atmospheric gas monitoring, and can be quantified directly via the <a href="/wiki/Eddy_covariance" title="Eddy covariance">eddy covariance</a> flux measurements,<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Monitoring_geological_sequestration_sites">Monitoring geological sequestration sites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Monitoring geological sequestration sites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In order to detect carbon dioxide leaks and the effectiveness of geological sequestration sites, different monitoring techniques can be employed to verify that the sequestered carbon stays trapped below the surface in the intended reservoir. Leakage due to injection at improper locations or conditions could result in carbon dioxide being released back into the atmosphere. It is important to be able to detect leaks with enough warning to put a stop to it, and to be able to quantify the amount of carbon that has leaked for purposes such as <a href="/wiki/Cap_and_trade" class="mw-redirect" title="Cap and trade">cap and trade</a> policies, evaluation of environmental impact of leaked carbon, as well as accounting for the total loss and cost of the process. To quantify the amount of carbon dioxide released, should a leak occur, or to closely watch stored CO<sub>2</sub>, there are several monitoring methods that can be done at both the surface and subsurface levels.<sup id="cite_ref-main_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-main-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsurface_monitoring">Subsurface monitoring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Subsurface monitoring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In subsurface monitoring, there are direct and indirect methods to determine the amount of CO<sub>2</sub> in the reservoir. A direct method would be drilling deep enough to collect a fluid sample. This drilling can be difficult and expensive due to the physical properties of the rock. It also only provides data at a specific location. Indirect methods would be to send sound or electromagnetic waves down to the reservoir where it is then reflected back up to be interpreted. This approach is also expensive but it provides data over a much larger region; it does however lack precision. Both direct and indirect monitoring can be done intermittently or continuously.<sup id="cite_ref-main_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-main-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seismic_monitoring">Seismic monitoring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Seismic monitoring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Seismometer" title="Seismometer">Seismic monitoring</a> is a type of indirect subsurface monitoring. It is done by creating vibrational waves either at the surface using a <a href="/wiki/Seismic_vibrator" title="Seismic vibrator">vibroseis truck</a>, or inside a well using <a href="/wiki/Rotating_unbalance" title="Rotating unbalance">spinning eccentric mass</a>. These vibrational waves then propagate through the geological layers and reflect back creating patterns that are read and interpreted by seismometers.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> It can identify migration pathways of the CO<sub>2</sub> plume.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Two examples of monitoring geological sequestration sites using seismic monitoring are the <a href="/wiki/Sleipner_gas_field" title="Sleipner gas field">Sleipner sequestration project</a> and the Frio CO<sub>2</sub> Injection test. Although this method can confirm the presence of CO<sub>2</sub> in a given region, it cannot determine the specifics of the environment or concentration of CO<sub>2</sub>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_monitoring">Surface monitoring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Surface monitoring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Eddy_covariance" title="Eddy covariance">Eddy covariance</a> is a surface monitoring technique that measures the flux of CO<sub>2</sub> from the ground's surface. It involves measuring CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations as well as vertical wind velocities using an anemometer.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> This provides a measure of the total vertical flux of CO2. Eddy covariance towers could potentially detect leaks, however, the natural carbon cycle, such as photosynthesis and the respiration of plants, would have to be accounted for and a baseline CO<sub>2</sub> cycle would have to be developed for the location of monitoring. An example of Eddy covariance techniques used to monitor carbon sequestration sites is the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://escholarship.org/uc/item/397007sh">Shallow Release test</a>. Another similar approach is utilizing accumulation chambers. These chambers are sealed to the ground with an inlet and outlet flow stream connected to a gas analyzer.<sup id="cite_ref-main_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-main-64">[64]</a></sup> This also measures the vertical flux of CO<sub>2</sub>. The disadvantage of accumulation chambers is its inability to monitor a large region which is necessary in detecting CO<sub>2</sub> leaks over the entire sequestration site.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="InSAR_monitoring">InSAR monitoring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: InSAR monitoring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/InSAR" class="mw-redirect" title="InSAR">InSAR</a> monitoring is another type of surface monitoring. It involves a satellite sending signals down to the Earth's surface where it is reflected back to the satellite's receiver. From this, the satellite is able to measure the distance to that point.<sup id="cite_ref-insar_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-insar-68">[68]</a></sup> In CCS, the injection of CO<sub>2</sub> in deep sublayers of geological sites creates high pressures. These high pressured, fluid filled layers affect those above and below it resulting in a change of the surface landscape. In areas of stored CO<sub>2</sub>, the ground's surface often rises due to the high pressures originating in the deep subsurface layers. These changes in elevation of the Earth's surface corresponds to a change in the distance from the inSAR satellite which is then detectable and measurable.<sup id="cite_ref-insar_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-insar-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_dioxide_recycling_-_carbon_capture_and_utilization_.28CCU.29">Carbon dioxide recycling - carbon capture and utilization (CCU)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Carbon dioxide recycling - carbon capture and utilization (CCU)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_neutral_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon neutral fuel">Carbon neutral fuel</a></div>
<p>Recycling CO<sub>2</sub> may offer a response to the global challenge of significantly reducing greenhouse gas emissions from major stationary (industrial) emitters in the near to medium term,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> but is usually considered a different technological category from CCS.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Technologies under development, such as Bio CCS Algal Synthesis,<sup id="cite_ref-globalccsinstitute.com_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-globalccsinstitute.com-70">[70]</a></sup> utilises pre-smokestack CO<sub>2</sub> (such as from a coal-fired power station) as a useful feedstock input to the production of oil-rich algae in solar membranes to produce oil for plastics and transport fuel (including aviation fuel), and nutritious stock-feed for farm animal production.<sup id="cite_ref-globalccsinstitute.com_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-globalccsinstitute.com-70">[70]</a></sup> The CO<sub>2</sub> and other captured greenhouse gases are injected into the membranes containing waste water and select strains of algae causing, together with sunlight or UV light, an oil rich biomass that doubles in mass every 24 hours<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.</p>
<p>The Bio CCS Algal Synthesis process is based on earth science photosynthesis: the technology is entirely retrofittable and collocated with the emitter, and the capital outlays may offer a return upon investment due to the high value commodities produced (oil for plastics, fuel and feed).</p>
<p>Bio CCS Algal Synthesis test facilities were being trialed at Australia's three largest coal-fired power stations (Tarong, Queensland; Eraring, NSW; Loy Yang, Victoria) using piped pre-emission smokestack CO<sub>2</sub> (and other greenhouse gases) as feedstock to grow oil-rich algal biomass in enclosed membranes for the production of plastics, transport fuel and nutritious animal feed.</p>
<p>Another potentially useful way of dealing with industrial sources of CO<sub>2</sub> is to convert it into hydrocarbons where it can be stored or reused as fuel or to make plastics. There are a number of projects investigating this possibility.<sup id="cite_ref-newsci2645_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newsci2645-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_scrubber" title="Carbon dioxide scrubber">Carbon dioxide scrubbing</a> variants exist based on <a href="/wiki/Potassium_carbonate" title="Potassium carbonate">potassium carbonate</a> which can be used to create liquid fuels, though this process requires a great deal of energy input.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Although the creation of fuel from atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> is not a <a href="/wiki/Climate_engineering" title="Climate engineering">climate engineering</a> technique, nor does it actually function as <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_remediation" class="mw-redirect" title="Greenhouse gas remediation">greenhouse gas remediation</a> (because carbon dioxide will be re-released when the fuel is burned) it nevertheless is potentially useful in the creation of a <a href="/wiki/Low_carbon" class="mw-redirect" title="Low carbon">low carbon</a> economy.</p>
<p>Other uses are the production of stable carbonates from silicates (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a> produces <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_carbonate" title="Magnesium carbonate">Magnesium carbonate</a>. This process is still in the R&amp;D phase.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Single_step_methods:_methanol">Single step methods: methanol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Single step methods: methanol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A proven process to produce a hydrocarbon is to make <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>. Methanol is easily synthesized from CO<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>. Based on this fact the idea of a <a href="/wiki/Methanol_economy" title="Methanol economy">methanol economy</a> was born.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Single_step_methods:_hydrocarbons">Single step methods: hydrocarbons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Single step methods: hydrocarbons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the department of Industrial Chemistry and Engineering of Materials at the University of Messina, Italy, there is a project to develop a system which works like a fuel-cell in reverse, whereby a catalyst is used that enables sunlight to split water into hydrogen ions and oxygen gas. The ions cross a membrane where they react with the CO<sub>2</sub> to create hydrocarbons.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Two_step_methods">Two step methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Two step methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>If CO<sub>2</sub> is heated to 2400&#160;°C, it splits into carbon monoxide (CO) and oxygen. The <a href="/wiki/Fischer-Tropsch_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Fischer-Tropsch process">Fischer-Tropsch process</a> can then be used to convert the CO into hydrocarbons. The required temperature can be achieved by using a chamber containing a mirror to focus sunlight on the gas. Rival teams are developing such chambers, at Solarec and at <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a>, both based in <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> According to Sandia these chambers could provide enough fuel to power 100% of domestic vehicles using 5800&#160;km<sup>2</sup>; unlike biofuels this would not take fertile land away from crops but would be land that is not being used for anything else. <a href="/wiki/James_May" title="James May">James May</a>, the British TV presenter, visited a demonstration plant in a programme in his <a href="/wiki/James_May%27s_Big_Ideas" title="James May's Big Ideas">Big Ideas</a> series.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Example_CCS_projects">Example CCS projects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Example CCS projects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial-scale_projects">Industrial-scale projects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Industrial-scale projects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of September 2012, the Global CCS Institute identified 75 large-scale integrated projects in its 2012 Global Status of CCS report<sup id="cite_ref-The_Global_CCS_Institute_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Global_CCS_Institute-76">[76]</a></sup> which is a net increase of one project since its 2011 Global Status of CCS report. 16 of these projects are in operation or in construction capturing around 36 million tonnes of CO<sub>2</sub> per annum.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Global_CCS_Institute_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Global_CCS_Institute-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> For more information see Integrated CCS Projects<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> on the Global CCS Institute's website. For information on EU projects see Zero Emissions Platform<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> website. The eight large-scale integrated CCS projects currently in operation are:</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Salah_CO2_Injection_.E2.80.94_Algeria">In Salah CO<sub>2</sub> Injection — <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: In Salah CO2 Injection — Algeria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/In_Salah" title="In Salah">In Salah</a> is a fully operational onshore gas field with CO<sub>2</sub> injection. CO<sub>2</sub> is separated from produced gas and reinjected in the producing hydrocarbon reservoir zones. Since 2004, about 1 Mt/a of CO<sub>2</sub> has been captured during <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> extraction and injected into the Krechba geologic formation at a depth of 1,800m. The Krechba formation is expected to store 17Mt CO<sub>2</sub> over the life of the project.</p>
<p>Injection suspended in 2011 due to concerns about the integrity of the seal.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sleipner_CO2_Injection_.E2.80.94_Norway">Sleipner CO<sub>2</sub> Injection — Norway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Sleipner CO2 Injection — Norway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sleipner_gas_field" title="Sleipner gas field">Sleipner gas field</a></div>
<p>Sleipner<sup id="cite_ref-Energy-pedia_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Energy-pedia-57">[57]</a></sup> is a fully operational offshore gas field with CO<sub>2</sub> injection initiated in 1996. CO<sub>2</sub> is separated from produced gas and reinjected in the Utsira saline aquifer (800–1000&#160;m below ocean floor) above the hydrocarbon reservoir zones. This aquifer extends much further north from the Sleipner facility at its southern extreme. The large size of the reservoir accounts for why 600 billion tonnes of CO<sub>2</sub> are expected to be stored, long after the Sleipner natural gas project has ended.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sn.C3.B8hvit_CO2_Injection_.E2.80.94_Norway">Snøhvit CO<sub>2</sub> Injection — Norway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Snøhvit CO2 Injection — Norway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Snøhvit is a fully operational offshore gas field with CO<sub>2</sub> injection. The LNG plant is located onshore. CO<sub>2</sub> is necessarily separated to produce <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_natural_gas" title="Liquefied natural gas">liquefied natural gas</a> (LNG) and then CO<sub>2</sub> is injected in a saline aquifer below the hydrocarbon reservoir zones offshore at a rate of 700,000 t/a into the Tubåen <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> formation 2,600 m under the seabed for storage. This formation was closed April 2011, and injection started in the Stø-formation where produced gas is taken. Produced CO<sub>2</sub> is increasing, therefore separation capacity may limit production before end 2015 when a new formation will be drilled for CO<sub>2</sub>-injection only. (Teknisk Ukeblad nr. 30, 2013, tu.no)</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Plains_Synfuel_Plant_and_Weyburn-Midale_Project_.E2.80.94_Canada">Great Plains Synfuel Plant and Weyburn-Midale Project — Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Great Plains Synfuel Plant and Weyburn-Midale Project — Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Weyburn-Midale is a <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">coal gasification</a> operation that produces synthetic natural gas and various petrochemicals from coal. This project captures about 2.8 Mt/a of CO<sub>2</sub> from its coal gasification plant located in <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>, US, transported by pipeline 320&#160;km across the Canada–US border and injects it into depleting oil fields in Saskatchewan where it is used for enhanced oil recovery (EOR).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Shute_Creek_Gas_Processing_Facility_.E2.80.94_US">Shute Creek Gas Processing Facility — US</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Shute Creek Gas Processing Facility — US">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Around 7 million tonnes per annum of carbon dioxide are recovered from <a href="/wiki/ExxonMobil" title="ExxonMobil">ExxonMobil</a>'s Shute Creek gas processing plant in <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a>, and transported by pipeline to various oil fields for enhanced oil recovery. This project has been operational since 1986.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Enid_Fertilizer_.E2.80.94_US">Enid Fertilizer — US</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Enid Fertilizer — US">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Enid Fertilizer plant sends 675,000 tonnes of CO<sub>2</sub> to be used for EOR. The pipeline and wells are operated separately by Anadarko Petroleum.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Val_Verde_Natural_Gas_Plants_.E2.80.94_US">Val Verde Natural Gas Plants — US</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Val Verde Natural Gas Plants — US">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>CO<sub>2</sub> from Mitchell, Gray Ranch, Puckett, and Turrell gas processing plants is transported via the Val Verde and CRC pipelines<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Naming_conventions_(geographic_names)" title="Wikipedia:Naming conventions (geographic names)"><span title="The geographic scope near this tag is ambiguous. (March 2014)">where?</span></a></i>]</sup> for EOR (incl. Sharon Ridge<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (March 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> EOR field).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Century_Plant_.E2.80.94_US">Century Plant — US</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Century Plant — US">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Occidental_Petroleum" title="Occidental Petroleum">Occidental Petroleum</a>, along with <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sandridge_Energy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sandridge Energy (page does not exist)">Sandridge Energy</a>, is operating a West Texas hydrocarbon gas processing plant and related pipeline infrastructure that provides CO<sub>2</sub> for use in <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_oil_recovery" title="Enhanced oil recovery">EOR</a>. With a total CO<sub>2</sub> capture capacity of 8.5 Mt/a expected in 2012, the Century plant would be the largest single industrial source CO<sub>2</sub> capture facility in North America.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Duke_Energy_East_Bend_Station_.E2.80.94_US">Duke Energy East Bend Station — US</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Duke Energy East Bend Station — US">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Researchers at the Center for Applied Energy Research of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Kentucky" title="University of Kentucky">University of Kentucky</a> are currently developing the algae-mediated conversion of coal-fired power plant flue gas to drop-in hydrocarbon fuels. Through their work, these researchers have proven that the carbon dioxide within <a href="/wiki/Flue_gas" title="Flue gas">flue gas</a> from <a href="/wiki/Coal-fired_power_plants" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal-fired power plants">coal-fired power plants</a> can be captured using algae, which can be subsequently harvested and utilized, e.g. as a feedstock for the production of drop-in hydrocarbon fuels.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The federal government in the 2008 and 2009 budgets has invested approximately $1.4 billion in Carbon Capture and Storage development.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alberta">Alberta</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Alberta">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Alberta has committed $170 million in 2013/2014 – and a total of $1.3 billion over 15 years – to fund two large-scale CCS projects that will help reduce CO<sub>2</sub> emissions from tar sands refining.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> In 2010 a grant agreement was signed with the Alberta Carbon Trunk Line.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The second is the Quest Project.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> The Quest project uses amine absorption to capture CO<sub>2</sub> from the Scotford Steam Methane Reformer units. Approximately 1.2 Mtonne CO<sub>2</sub> per year is captured and transported through 64&#160;km of onshore pipeline into a saline aquifer in the Cambrian Basal Sands.<sup id="cite_ref-Quest_Carbon_Capture_and_Storage_Project_Annual_Summary_Report_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quest_Carbon_Capture_and_Storage_Project_Annual_Summary_Report-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Columbia">British Columbia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: British Columbia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Spectra_Energy" title="Spectra Energy">Spectra Energy</a>'s Fort Nelson Project is proposed but still needs to secure funding.<sup id="cite_ref-Canadian_CCS_Projects_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canadian_CCS_Projects-88">[88]</a></sup> The total project cost is estimated to be US $12.5 Million. The source of CO<sub>2</sub> will be from the Fort Nelson Natural Gas Processing Plant and will be transported 15&#160;km via an onshore pipeline to middle Devonian carbonate rock that is between 6500 and 7000 feet deep. The process will capture 2.2 Megatonne CO<sub>2</sub> per year using an amine process in its pre-combustion capture. Injections and MVA Operations have already occurred (2014) and is projected to startup in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-Spectra_Energy.27s_Fort_Nelson_CCS_Project_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spectra_Energy.27s_Fort_Nelson_CCS_Project-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Saskatchewan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Led by the province's full-service utility, SaskPower, one of the world's first and largest full production carbon capture facilities is operating at <a href="/wiki/Boundary_Dam_Power_Station" title="Boundary Dam Power Station">Boundary Dam Power Station</a>. With an initial investment of $1.5 to $1.6 billion,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> SaskPower will be generating a revenue by selling a portion of the captured CO<sub>2</sub> back into the market to be used for enhanced oil recovery. The project started in May 2011 and became operational in October 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The post-combustion full flue gas capture process will capture 1 million tonnes of CO<sub>2</sub> a year.<sup id="cite_ref-Canadian_CCS_Projects_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canadian_CCS_Projects-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pilot_projects">Pilot projects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Pilot projects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Alberta Saline Aquifer Project (ASAP), Husky Upgrader and Ethanol Plant pilot, Heartland Area Redwater Project (HARP), Wabamun Area Sequestration Project(WASP), and Aquistore.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another Canadian initiative is the Integrated CO<sub>2</sub> Network (ICO<sub>2</sub>N), a group of industry participants providing a framework for carbon capture and storage development in Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Other Canadian organizations related to CCS include CCS 101, Carbon Management Canada, IPAC CO<sub>2</sub>, and the Canadian Clean Power Coalition.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Netherlands">Netherlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Netherlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the Netherlands, a 68-megawatt oxyfuel plant ("Zero Emission Power Plant") was being planned to be operational in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> This project was later canceled.</p>
<p>ROAD (Rotterdam Capture and Storage Demonstration project) is a joint project by E.ON Benelux and Electrabel Nederland / GDF SUEZ Group. Every year, starting in 2015 ROAD will capture around 1.1 million tonnes of CO<sub>2</sub> at the new power plant on the Maasvlakte. This will be stored in depleted gas reservoirs under the North Sea.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>Developed in the Netherlands, an electrocatalysis by a copper complex helps <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">reduce carbon dioxide</a> to <a href="/wiki/Oxalic_acid" title="Oxalic acid">oxalic acid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bouwman_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bouwman-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
