<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Norway">Norway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Norway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Norway, the CO<sub>2</sub> Technology Centre (TCM) at <a href="/wiki/Mongstad" title="Mongstad">Mongstad</a> began construction in 2009, and completed in 2012. It includes two capture technology plants (one advanced amine and one chilled ammonia), both capturing fluegas from two sources. This includes a gas-fired power plant and refinery cracker fluegas (similar to coal-fired power plant fluegas).</p>
<p>In addition to this, the Mongstad site was also planned to have a full-scale CCS demonstration plant. The project was delayed to 2014, 2018, and then indefinitely.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The project cost rose to USD 985 million.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Then in October 2011, Aker Solutions' wrote off its investment in Aker Clean Carbon, declaring the carbon sequestration market to be "dead".<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 1 October 2013 Norway asked <a href="/wiki/Gassnova" title="Gassnova">Gassnova</a> not to sign any contracts for Carbon capture and storage outside Mongstad.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poland">Poland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Poland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Belchatów, Poland,<sup id="cite_ref-https:.2F.2Fwww.ccsnetwork.eu_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-https:.2F.2Fwww.ccsnetwork.eu-102">[102]</a></sup> a lignite-fired energy plant of more than 858 MW is planned to be in operation in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In November 2008, the DOE awarded a $66.9 million eight-year grant to a research partnership headed by <a href="/wiki/Montana_State_University_%E2%80%93_Bozeman" class="mw-redirect" title="Montana State University – Bozeman">Montana State University</a> to demonstrate that underground geologic formations "can store huge volumes of carbon dioxide economically, safely and permanently".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Researchers under the Big Sky Regional Carbon Sequestration Project plan to inject up to one million tonnes of CO<sub>2</sub> into <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> beneath southwestern Wyoming.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the United States, four different <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fuel" title="Synthetic fuel">synthetic fuel</a> projects are moving forward, which have publicly announced plans to incorporate carbon capture and storage:</p>
<ol>
<li>American Clean Coal Fuels, in their Illinois Clean Fuels (ICF) project, is developing a 30,000-barrel (4,800&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) per day biomass and <a href="/wiki/Coal_to_liquids" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal to liquids">coal to liquids</a> project in <a href="/wiki/Oakland,_Illinois" title="Oakland, Illinois">Oakland, Illinois</a>, which will market the CO<sub>2</sub> created at the plant for enhanced oil recovery applications. By combining sequestration and <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> feedstocks, the ICF project will achieve dramatic reductions in the life-cycle carbon footprint of the fuels they produce. If sufficient biomass is used, the plant should have the capability to go life-cycle carbon negative, meaning that effectively, for each gallon of their fuel that is used, carbon is pulled out of the air, and put into the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li>Baard Energy, in their Ohio River Clean Fuels project, is developing a 53,000&#160;bbl/d (8,400&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d) coal and biomass to liquids project, which has announced plans to market the plant's CO<sub>2</sub> for enhanced oil recovery.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li>Rentech is developing a 29,600-barrel (4,710&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) per day coal and biomass to liquids plant in <a href="/wiki/Natchez,_Mississippi" title="Natchez, Mississippi">Natchez, Mississippi</a>, which will market the plant's CO<sub>2</sub> for enhanced oil recovery. The first phase of the project is expected in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></li>
<li>DKRW<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (March 2011)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> is developing a 15,000–20,000-barrel (2,400–3,200&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) per day <a href="/wiki/Coal_to_liquids" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal to liquids">coal to liquids</a> plant in <a href="/wiki/Medicine_Bow,_Wyoming" title="Medicine Bow, Wyoming">Medicine Bow, Wyoming</a>, which will market its plant's CO<sub>2</sub> for <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_oil_recovery" title="Enhanced oil recovery">enhanced oil recovery</a>. The project is expected to begin operation in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>In October 2009, the U.S. Department of Energy awarded grants to twelve Industrial Carbon Capture and Storage (ICCS) projects to conduct a Phase 1 feasibility study.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The DOE plans to select 3 to 4 of those projects to proceed into Phase 2, design and construction, with operational startup to occur by 2015. <a href="/wiki/Battelle_Memorial_Institute" title="Battelle Memorial Institute">Battelle Memorial Institute</a>, Pacific Northwest Division, Boise, Inc., and <a href="/wiki/Fluor_Corporation" title="Fluor Corporation">Fluor Corporation</a> are studying a CCS system for capture and storage of CO<sub>2</sub> emissions associated with the pulp and paper production industry. The site of the study is the Boise White Paper L.L.C. paper mill located near the township of Wallula in Southeastern Washington State. The plant generates approximately 1.2 MMT of CO<sub>2</sub> annually from a set of three recovery boilers that are mainly fired with black liquor, a recycled byproduct formed during the pulping of wood for paper-making. Fluor Corporation will design a customized version of their Econamine Plus carbon capture technology. The Fluor system also will be designed to remove residual quantities of remnant air pollutants from stack gases as part of the CO<sub>2</sub> capture process. Battelle is leading preparation of an Environmental Information Volume (EIV) for the entire project, including geologic storage of the captured CO<sub>2</sub> in deep flood basalt formations that exist in the greater region. The EIV will describe the necessary site characterization work, sequestration system infrastructure, and monitoring program to support permanent sequestration of the CO<sub>2</sub> captured at the plant.</p>
<p>In addition to individual carbon capture and sequestration projects, there are a number of U.S. programs designed to research, develop, and deploy CCS technologies on a broad scale. These include the National Energy Technology Laboratory's (NETL) Carbon Sequestration Program, regional carbon sequestration partnerships and the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_Sequestration_Leadership_Forum" title="Carbon Sequestration Leadership Forum">Carbon Sequestration Leadership Forum</a> (CSLF).<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="SECARB">SECARB</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: SECARB">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In October 2007, the Bureau of Economic Geology at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a> received a 10-year, $38 million subcontract to conduct the first intensively monitored long-term project in the United States studying the feasibility of injecting a large volume of CO<sub>2</sub> for underground storage.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> The project is a research program of the Southeast Regional Carbon Sequestration Partnership (SECARB), funded by the <a href="/wiki/National_Energy_Technology_Laboratory" title="National Energy Technology Laboratory">National Energy Technology Laboratory</a> of the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Energy" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Department of Energy">U.S. Department of Energy</a> (DOE).</p>
<p>The SECARB partnership will demonstrate CO<sub>2</sub> injection rate and storage capacity in the Tuscaloosa-Woodbine geologic system that stretches from Texas to Florida. The region has the potential to store more than 200 billion tons<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (September 2008)">vague</span></a></i>]</sup> of CO<sub>2</sub> from major point sources in the region, equal to about 33 years of overall U.S. emissions at present rates. Beginning in fall 2007, the project will inject CO<sub>2</sub> at the rate of one million tons<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (September 2008)">vague</span></a></i>]</sup> per year, for up to 1.5 years, into brine up to 10,000 feet (3,000 m) below the land surface near the Cranfield oil field, which lays about 15 miles (24&#160;km) east of <a href="/wiki/Natchez,_Mississippi" title="Natchez, Mississippi">Natchez, Mississippi</a>. Experimental equipment will measure the ability of the subsurface to accept and retain CO<sub>2</sub>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kemper_Project">Kemper Project</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Kemper Project">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kemper_Project" title="Kemper Project">Kemper Project</a>, is an electrical generating station currently under construction in <a href="/wiki/Kemper_County,_Mississippi" title="Kemper County, Mississippi">Kemper County, Mississippi</a>. <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Power" title="Mississippi Power">Mississippi Power</a>, a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/Southern_Company" title="Southern Company">Southern Company</a>, began construction of the plant in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> The project is central to President Obama's Climate Plan.<sup id="cite_ref-kemp2016_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kemp2016-115">[115]</a></sup> Once operational, the Kemper Project will be a first-of-its-kind electricity plant to employ gasification and carbon capture technologies at this scale.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, there have been project management problems.<sup id="cite_ref-kemp2016_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kemp2016-115">[115]</a></sup> The power plant construction has been delayed and is scheduled to open in the third quarter of 2016, more than two years behind schedule, at a cost of $6.6 billion—three times original cost estimate.<sup id="cite_ref-Amy2_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amy2-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ABC_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABC-118">[118]</a></sup> According to a <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Club" title="Sierra Club">Sierra Club</a> analysis, Kemper is the most expensive power plant ever built for the watts of electricity it will generate.<sup id="cite_ref-Drajem_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drajem-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Texas_Clean_Energy_Project">Texas Clean Energy Project</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Texas Clean Energy Project">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Texas Clean Energy Project (TCEP) is a "NowGen" <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Gasification_Combined_Cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Integrated Gasification Combined Cycle">Integrated Gasification Combined Cycle</a> (IGCC) facility that will incorporate carbon capture, utilization and storage (CCUS) technology in a first-of-its-kind commercial <a href="/wiki/Clean_coal" class="mw-redirect" title="Clean coal">clean coal</a> power plant. This project is expected to be operational in 2018. It will be the first US-based power plant to combine both IGCC and capture 90% of its emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Big_Brown_Steam_Electric_Station">Big Brown Steam Electric Station</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Big Brown Steam Electric Station">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Examples of carbon sequestration at an existing US coal plant can be found at utility company <a href="/wiki/Luminant" title="Luminant">Luminant</a>'s pilot version at its <a href="/w/index.php?title=Big_Brown_Steam_Electric_Station&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Big Brown Steam Electric Station (page does not exist)">Big Brown Steam Electric Station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fairfield,_Texas" title="Fairfield, Texas">Fairfield, Texas</a>. This system is converting carbon from smokestacks into <a href="/wiki/Baking_soda" class="mw-redirect" title="Baking soda">baking soda</a>. Skyonic plans to circumvent storage problems of liquid CO<sub>2</sub> by storing baking soda in mines, landfills, to be sold as industrial or food grade baking soda,<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Green Fuel Technologies is piloting and implementing algae based carbon capture, circumventing storage issues by then converting algae into fuel or feed,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> though this may lead to re-release of the carbon.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The government of the United Kingdom launched a first tender process for a CCS demonstration project in 2007. The project were to use post-combustion technology on coal-fired power generation at 300–400&#160;megawatts or equivalent. The project aimed to be operational by 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> The Government announced in June 2008 that four companies had pre-qualified for the competition: BP Alternative Energy International Limited, EON UK Plc, Peel Power Limited and Scottish Power Generation Limited.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> BP subsequently withdrew from the competition, claiming it could not find a power generator partner, and RWE <a href="/wiki/Npower_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Npower (UK)">npower</a> sought a judicial review of the process after it did not qualify.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> This first CCS tender was cancelled in late 2011 when government could not reach agreement with the ScottishPower/Shell/National Grid consortium on terms and cost, for the project based on retrofitting the existing Longannet coal-fired power station in Scotland.</p>
<p>A second tender process was launched by government in 2012 as part of DECC's CCS Commercialisation Programme and two bidders, namely the Shell Peterhead gas-fired power station CCS project and the White Rose CCS project based upon a new oxy-fuel coal-fired unit at Drax power station were selected in 2013 to proceed to a funded front-end engineering and design phase. This second tender was cancelled in November 2015 following a government spending review at the time of the Chancellor's Autumn Statement.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>Doosan Babcock has modified their Clean Combustion Test Facility (CCTF) in Renfrew, Scotland to create the largest Oxyfuel test facility currently in the world.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Oxyfuel firing on pulverized coal with recycled flue gas demonstrates the operation of a full scale 40 MW burner for use in coal-fired boilers. Sponsors of the project include the UK Department for Business Enterprise and Regulatory Reform (BERR,) as well as a group of industrial sponsors and university partners comprising Scottish and Southern Energy (Prime Sponsor), E.ON UK PLC, Drax Power Limited, ScottishPower, EDF Energy, Dong Energy Generation, Air Products Plc (Sponsors), and Imperial College and University of Nottingham (University Partners).<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009 UK firm 2Co Energy was awarded planning permission for a £5bn power station and carbon-capture-and-storage project at Hatfield, near Doncaster and £164m of EU funding. Technology company Samsung agreed to take a 15% stake in the project.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> It is planned to construct a 60&#160;km (37&#160;mi) pipeline from Stainforth, near Hatfield in South Yorkshire to Barmston in the East Riding of Yorkshire. CO<sub>2</sub> would then be stored in natural porous rock beneath the North Sea. National Grid believes the project has the potential to reduce CO<sub>2</sub> emissions from power stations across Yorkshire and the Humber by up to 90% and both the proposed White Rose CCS project at Drax Power Station in North Yorkshire and the proposed Don Valley Power Project at Hatfield could benefit from the scheme.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Northeast_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Northeast of England">Northeast of England</a>, The <a href="/wiki/Northeast_of_England_Process_Industry_Cluster" class="mw-redirect" title="Northeast of England Process Industry Cluster">Northeast of England Process Industry Cluster</a> (NEPIC) of commodity chemical manufacturers are amongst the largest single point producers of carbon dioxide in the United Kingdom and they have created within NEPIC the Process Industry Carbon Capture and Storage Initiative<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> (PICCSI) to study the possibility of a carbon capture and storage (CCS) solution being provided for the chemical and steel manufacturing industry on <a href="/wiki/Teesside" title="Teesside">Teesside</a>, as well as for any carbon based energy production. This CCS technology option is being considered as a result of climate change regulations and the carbon taxation that could become a prohibitive cost for such energy intensive industries.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Crown_Estate" title="Crown Estate">Crown Estate</a> is responsible for storage rights on the UK continental shelf and it has facilitated work on offshore carbon dioxide storage technical and commercial issues.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Beijing, as of 2009, one major power plant is capturing and re-selling a small fraction of its CO<sub>2</sub> emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The German industrial area of Schwarze Pumpe, about 4 kilometres (2.5&#160;mi) south of the city of <a href="/wiki/Spremberg" title="Spremberg">Spremberg</a>, is home to the world's first demonstration CCS coal plant, the <a href="/wiki/Schwarze_Pumpe_power_station" title="Schwarze Pumpe power station">Schwarze Pumpe power station</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> The mini pilot plant is run by an <a href="/wiki/Alstom" title="Alstom">Alstom</a>-built <a href="/wiki/Oxy-fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxy-fuel">oxy-fuel</a> boiler and is also equipped with a flue gas cleaning facility to remove <a href="/wiki/Fly_ash" title="Fly ash">fly ash</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulphur_dioxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Sulphur dioxide">sulphur dioxide</a>. The Swedish company <a href="/wiki/Vattenfall_AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Vattenfall AB">Vattenfall AB</a> invested some €70&#160;million in the two-year project, which began operation September 9, 2008. The power plant, which is rated at 30 <a href="/wiki/Megawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Megawatt">megawatts</a>, is a pilot project to serve as a prototype for future full-scale power plants.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> 240 tonnes a day of CO<sub>2</sub> are being trucked 350 kilometers (220&#160;mi) where it will be injected into an empty gas field. Germany's <a href="/wiki/Bund_f%C3%BCr_Umwelt_und_Naturschutz_Deutschland" title="Bund für Umwelt und Naturschutz Deutschland">BUND group</a> called it a "<a href="/wiki/Fig_leaf" title="Fig leaf">fig leaf</a>". For each tonne of coal burned, 3.6 tonnes of carbon dioxide is produced.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> The CCS program at Schwarze Pumpe ended in 2014 due to inviable costs and energy use.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>German utility <a href="/wiki/RWE" title="RWE">RWE</a> operates a pilot-scale CO<sub>2</sub> scrubber at the lignite-fired <a href="/wiki/Niederau%C3%9Fem_power_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Niederaußem power station">Niederaußem power station</a> built in cooperation with <a href="/wiki/BASF" title="BASF">BASF</a> (supplier of detergent) and <a href="/wiki/The_Linde_Group" title="The Linde Group">Linde</a> engineering.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Jänschwalde, Germany,<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> a plan is in the works for an Oxyfuel boiler, rated at 650 thermal MW (around 250 electric MW), which is about 20 times more than Vattenfall's 30 MW pilot plant under construction, and compares to today's largest Oxyfuel test rigs of 0.5 MW. Post-combustion capture technology will also be demonstrated at Jänschwalde.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage_in_Australia" title="Carbon capture and storage in Australia">Carbon capture and storage in Australia</a></div>
<p>The Federal Resources and Energy Minister Martin Ferguson opened the first geosequestration project in the southern hemisphere in April 2008. The demonstration plant is near Nirranda South in South Western Victoria. (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;params=35.31_S_149.14_E_"><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">35°19′S</span> <span class="longitude">149°08′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">35.31°S 149.14°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">-35.31; 149.14</span></span></span></a></span>) The plant is owned by the Cooperative Research Centre for Greenhouse Gas Technologies (CO2CRC). CO2CRC is a non profit research collaboration supported by government and industry. The project has stored and monitored over 65,000 tonnes of carbon dioxide-rich gas which was extracted from a natural gas reservoir via a well, compressed and piped 2.25&#160;km to a new well. There the gas has been injected into a depleted natural gas reservoir approximately two kilometers below the surface.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> The project has moved to a second stage and is investigating carbon dioxide trapping in a saline aquifer 1500 meters below the surface. The Otway Project is a research and demonstration project, focused on comprehensive monitoring and verification.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>This plant does not propose to capture CO<sub>2</sub> from coal-fired power generation, though two CO2CRC demonstration projects at a Victorian power station and research gasifier are demonstrating solvent, membrane, and adsorbent capture technologies from coal combustion.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Currently, only small-scale projects are storing CO<sub>2</sub> stripped from the products of combustion of coal burnt for electricity generation at coal-fired <a href="/wiki/Power_stations" class="mw-redirect" title="Power stations">power stations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Work currently being carried out by the GreenMag Group and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Newcastle_(Australia)" title="University of Newcastle (Australia)">University of Newcastle</a> and funded by the <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a> and Australian Governments and industry intends to have a working mineral carbonation pilot plant in operation by 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-www.dpi.nsw.gov.au_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-www.dpi.nsw.gov.au-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>View the full list of Zero Emission Projects for fossil fuel power plant in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Limitations_of_CCS_for_power_stations">Limitations of CCS for power stations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Limitations of CCS for power stations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Critics say large-scale CCS deployment is unproven and decades away from being commercialized. They say that it is risky and expensive and that a better option is <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>. Some environmental groups point out that CCS technology leaves behind dangerous waste material that has to be stored, just like nuclear power stations.<sup id="cite_ref-ti12_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ti12-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another limitation of CCS is its energy penalty. The technology is expected to use between 10 and 40 percent of the energy produced by a power station.<sup id="cite_ref-falsehope_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-falsehope-151">[151]</a></sup> Wide-scale adoption of CCS may erase efficiency gains in coal power plants of the last 50 years, and increase resource consumption by one third. Even taking the fuel penalty into account, however, overall levels of CO<sub>2</sub> abatement would remain high at approximately 80–90%, compared to a plant without CCS.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> It is possible for CCS, when combined with biomass, to result in net negative emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Though, all of the currently (as of Feb 2011) operational BECCS (<a href="/wiki/Bio-energy_with_carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Bio-energy with carbon capture and storage">Bio-energy with carbon capture and storage</a>) plants operate on point emissions other than power stations, such as <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a> refineries.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>The use of CCS can reduce CO<sub>2</sub> emissions from the stacks of coal power plants by 85–90% or more, but it has no effect on CO<sub>2</sub> emissions due to the mining and transport of coal. It will actually "increase such emissions and of air pollutants per unit of net delivered power and will increase all ecological, land-use, air-pollution, and water-pollution impacts from coal mining, transport, and processing, because the CCS system requires 25% more energy, thus 25% more coal combustion, than does a system without CCS".<sup id="cite_ref-WWS2010_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WWS2010-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another concern regards the permanence of storage schemes. Opponents to CCS claim that safe and permanent storage of CO<sub>2</sub> cannot be guaranteed and that even very low leakage rates could undermine any climate mitigation effect.<sup id="cite_ref-falsehope_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-falsehope-151">[151]</a></sup> In 1986 a large leakage of naturally sequestered CO<sub>2</sub> rose from <a href="/wiki/Lake_Nyos" title="Lake Nyos">Lake Nyos</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> and asphyxiated 1,700 people. While the carbon had been sequestered naturally, some point to the event as evidence for the potentially catastrophic effects of sequestering carbon artificially.<sup id="cite_ref-forbes.com_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forbes.com-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ti12_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ti12-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>On one hand, <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a> claims that CCS could lead to a doubling of coal plant costs.<sup id="cite_ref-falsehope_151-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-falsehope-151">[151]</a></sup> It is also claimed by opponents to CCS that money spent on CCS will divert investments away from other solutions to climate change. On the other hand, CCS is pointed out as economically attractive in comparison to other forms of low carbon electricity generation and seen by the <a href="/wiki/IPCC" class="mw-redirect" title="IPCC">IPCC</a> and others as a critical component for meeting <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation_scenarios" title="Climate change mitigation scenarios">mitigation targets</a> such as 450 ppm and 350 ppm.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cost">Cost</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Cost">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although the processes involved in CCS have been demonstrated in other industrial applications, no commercial scale projects which integrate these processes exist; the costs therefore are somewhat uncertain. Some recent credible estimates indicate that the cost of capturing and storing carbon dioxide is US$60 per ton,<sup id="cite_ref-Science323_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Science323-159">[159]</a></sup> corresponding to an increase in electricity prices of about US 6c per kWh (based on typical coal-fired power plant emissions of 0.97&#160;kg (2.13&#160;lb) CO<sub>2</sub> per kWh). This would double the typical US industrial electricity price (now at around 6c per kWh) and increase the typical retail residential electricity price by about 50% (assuming 100% of power is from coal, which may not necessarily be the case, as this varies from state to state). Similar (approximate) price increases would likely be expected in coal dependent countries such as Australia, because the capture technology and chemistry, as well as the transport and injection costs from such power plants would not, in an overall sense, vary significantly from country to country.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The reasons that CCS is expected to cause such power price increases are several. Firstly, the increased energy requirements of capturing and compressing CO<sub>2</sub> significantly raises the operating costs of CCS-equipped power plants. In addition, there are added investment and capital costs. The process would increase the fuel requirement of a plant with CCS by about 25% for a coal-fired plant, and about 15% for a gas-fired plant.<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_CC_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_CC-4">[4]</a></sup> The cost of this extra fuel, as well as storage and other system costs, are estimated to increase the costs of energy from a power plant with CCS by 30–60%, depending on the specific circumstances. Pre-commercial CCS demonstration projects are likely to be more expensive than mature CCS technology; the total additional costs of an early large-scale CCS demonstration project are estimated to be €0.5-1.1 billion per project over the project lifetime. Other applications are possible. In the belief that use of sequestered carbon could be harnessed to offset the cost of capture and storage, Walker Architects published the first CO<sub>2</sub> gas <a href="/wiki/Compressed_air_energy_storage" title="Compressed air energy storage">CAES</a> application, proposing the use of sequestered CO<sub>2</sub> for Energy Storage on October 24, 2008. To date the feasibility of such potential offsets to the cost have not been examined.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>An estimate of costs of energy with and without CCS (2002 US$ per <a href="/wiki/KWh" class="mw-redirect" title="KWh">kWh</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_CC_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_CC-4">[4]</a></sup></caption>
<tr style="background:#ececec;">
<td></td>
<td><b>Natural gas combined cycle</b></td>
<td><b>Pulverized coal</b></td>
<td><b>Integrated gasification combined cycle</b></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Without capture (reference plant)</td>
<td>0.03–0.05</td>
<td>0.04–0.05</td>
<td>0.04–0.06</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>With capture and geological storage</td>
<td>0.04–0.08</td>
<td>0.06–0.10</td>

<td>0.02–0.05</td>
<td>0.02–0.03</td>

<tr>

<td>0.04–0.07</td>
<td>0.05–0.08</td>
<td>0.04–0.08</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="tNote" colspan="7">All costs refer to costs for energy from newly built, large-scale plants. Natural gas combined cycle costs are based on natural gas prices of US$2.80–4.40 per GJ (<a href="/wiki/Lower_heating_value" class="mw-redirect" title="Lower heating value">LHV</a> based). Energy costs for PC and IGCC are based on <a href="/wiki/Bituminous_coal" title="Bituminous coal">bituminous coal</a> costs of US$1.00–1.50 per GJ <a href="/wiki/Lower_heating_value" class="mw-redirect" title="Lower heating value">LHV</a>. Note that the costs are very dependent on fuel prices (which change continuously), in addition to other factors such as capital costs. Also note that for EOR, the savings are greater for higher oil prices. Current gas and oil prices are substantially higher than the figures used here. All figures in the table are from Table 8.3a in [IPCC, 2005].<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_CC_4-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_CC-4">[4]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The cost of CCS depends on the cost of capture and storage, which varies according to the method used. Geological storage in saline formations or depleted oil or gas fields typically cost US$0.50–8.00 per tonne of CO<sub>2</sub> injected, plus an additional US$0.10–0.30 for monitoring costs. When storage is combined with <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_oil_recovery" title="Enhanced oil recovery">enhanced oil recovery</a> to extract extra oil from an oil field, however, the storage could yield net benefits of US$10–16 per tonne of CO<sub>2</sub> injected (based on 2003 oil prices). This would likely negate some of the effect of the carbon capture when the oil was burnt as fuel. Even taking this into account, as the table above shows, the benefits do not outweigh the extra costs of capture.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Cost of electricity generated by different sources including those incorporating CCS technologies can be found in <a href="/wiki/Cost_of_electricity_by_source" title="Cost of electricity by source">cost of electricity by source</a>. If CO<sub>2</sub> capture was part of a fuel cycle then the CO<sub>2</sub> would have value rather than be a cost. The proposed Solar Fuel or methane cycle proposed by the <a href="/wiki/Fraunhofer_Society" title="Fraunhofer Society">Fraunhofer Society</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> amongst others is an example. This "solar fuel"<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> cycle uses the excess electrical renewable energy to create hydrogen via electrolysis of water.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> The hydrogen is then combined with CO<sub>2</sub> to create synthetic natural gas SNG and stored in the gas network. See the latest Cost Report on the Cost of CO<sub>2</sub> Capture produced by the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.zeroemissionsplatform.eu/library/publication/166-zep-cost-report-capture.html">Zero Emissions Platform</a></p>
<p>Governments around the world have provided a range of different types of funding support to CCS demonstration projects, including tax credits, allocations and grants. The funding is associated with both a desire to accelerate innovation activities for CCS as a low-carbon technology and the need for economic stimulus activities. As of 2011, approximately US$23.5bn has been made available to support large-scale CCS demonstration projects around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_capture_and_storage_and_the_Kyoto_Protocol">Carbon capture and storage and the Kyoto Protocol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Carbon capture and storage and the Kyoto Protocol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One way to finance future CCS projects could be through the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Development_Mechanism" title="Clean Development Mechanism">Clean Development Mechanism</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a>. At <a href="/wiki/COP16" class="mw-redirect" title="COP16">COP16</a> in 2010, The Subsidiary Body for Scientific and Technological Advice, at its thirty-third session, issued a draft document recommending the inclusion of Carbon dioxide capture and storage in geological formations in Clean Development Mechanism project activities.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/COP17" class="mw-redirect" title="COP17">COP17</a> in <a href="/wiki/Durban" title="Durban">Durban</a>, a final agreement was reached enabling CCS projects to receive support through the Clean Development Mechanism.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_effects">Environmental effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Environmental effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carbon_capture_and_storage&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(January 2009)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The theoretical merit of CCS systems is the reduction of CO<sub>2</sub> emissions by up to 90%, depending on plant type. Generally, environmental effects from use of CCS arise during power production, CO<sub>2</sub> capture, transport, and storage. Issues relating to storage are discussed in those sections.</p>
<p>Additional energy is required for CO<sub>2</sub> capture, and this means that substantially more fuel has to be used to produce the same amount of power, depending on the plant type. For new super-critical pulverized coal (PC) plants using current technology, the extra energy requirements range from 24 to 40%, while for natural gas combined cycle (NGCC) plants the range is 11–22% and for coal-based gasification combined cycle (IGCC) systems it is 14–25% [IPCC, 2005].<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Obviously, fuel use and environmental problems arising from mining and extraction of coal or gas increase accordingly. Plants equipped with <a href="/wiki/Flue-gas_desulfurization" title="Flue-gas desulfurization">flue-gas desulfurization</a> (FGD) systems for <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> control require proportionally greater amounts of <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>, and systems equipped with <a href="/wiki/Selective_catalytic_reduction" title="Selective catalytic reduction">selective catalytic reduction</a> systems for <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">nitrogen oxides produced during combustion</a> require proportionally greater amounts of <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>.</p>
<p>IPCC has provided estimates of air emissions from various CCS plant designs (see table below). While CO<sub>2</sub> is drastically reduced though never completely captured, emissions of air pollutants increase significantly, generally due to the energy penalty of capture. Hence, the use of CCS entails a reduction in air quality. Type and amount of air pollutants still depends on technology. CO<sub>2</sub> is captured with alkaline solvents catching the acidic CO<sub>2</sub> at low temperatures in the absorber and releasing CO<sub>2</sub> at higher temperatures in a desorber. Chilled Ammonia CCS Plants have inevitable ammonia emissions to air. "Functionalized Ammonia" emit less ammonia, but amines may form secondary amines and these will emit volatile nitrosamines<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> by a side reaction with nitrogendioxide, which is present in any flue gas even after DeNOx. Nevertheless, there are advanced amines in testing with little to no vapor pressure to avoid these amine- and consecutive nitrosamine emissions. Nevertheless, all the capture plants amines have in common, that practically 100% of remaining sulfur dioxide from the plant is washed out of the flue gas, the same applies to dust/ash.</p>

<td><b>Integrated gasification combined cycle</b></td>
</tr>

<td>0.43 (-89%)</td>
<td>1.07 (−87%)</td>
<td>0.97 (−88%)</td>
</tr>
<tr>

</tr>

<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.scientificamerican.com/report.cfm?id=carbon-capture-storage-ccs">A Guide To Carbon Capture And Storage: Can carbon capture and storage save the climate from the consequences of fossil fuel burning?</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.powerplantccs.com">Powerplantccs Power Plant Carbon Capture, Storage, CO<sub>2</sub> Sequestration</a></li>
